Round 6 — Pricing and economics

The question on the table

Round 5 locked the packaging structure and deliberately deferred every price point to here. So Round 6 does not relitigate the catalog — C1–C5 (core) and P-1 (perimeter) are fixed (R05 D13). It does the one thing Round 5 refused to do: put numbers on the catalog, commit the value metric to a fee-to-value ratio, design the anchoring and expansion mechanics, and then submit the whole pricing system to P11's spreadsheet to see whether the unit economics and the capacity survive it.

The round inherits four binding constraints it is not allowed to break (carried in the ledger's next_unit):

  1. Price points stay COI-capped Medium. Every independent number desk research can reach is a seller's rate card (R03 A); the cap is not liftable by more searching — only a buyer pricing probe lifts it (R03 #1). So this round commits numbers inside the accepted band and gates each on that probe. It does not pretend the numbers are validated.
  2. Price the wedge above the compressing commodity band. The specialist/generalist gap is decade-wide — generalist ~$250/hr vs cyber/AI specialist $600+/hr (R03 C). The core is priced on that premium; pricing it at the commodity floor forfeits the wedge and the margin.
  3. Two segment-specific value metrics are already locked (R03 B; R05 D15): SaaS = revenue/deal unblocked; bank = examiner defensibility + avoided AMP. The practical structure is hybrid (base retainer + outcome anchor); pure outcome-pricing is hard to operationalize.
  4. The per-advisor model is a knife-edge on two variables — utilization AND premium pricing (R05 B). WNW is supply-bound, not demand-/CAC-/LTV-bound; LTV:CAC clears 3:1 with room (~7–20×, R05 E). The pricing must hold the knife-edge at achievable volume, and the per-advisor numbers are a pilot hypothesis until WNW internal data returns.

The forced disagreement is built into the roster: - P8 (Pricing Architect) pushes the value metric, the anchoring, and the expansion mechanics — the boutique's money is in the ladder and the premium, not the logo. - P11 (Capacity Modeler) holds the economic floor and the veto: does the price hold the knife-edge at achievable volume? — which binds the open T2 bridge-core-size fork. - P3 (Operator) grounds every price in delivery cost and the effective senior-hour realization after pod leverage. - P1 (SaaS Buyer) resists anything that reads as open-ended, or as commodity work priced up. - P2 (Regulated Buyer) pays for examiner-survivable judgment, not commodity hours — and the spend must be examiner-justifiable (the FDIC caution).

There is no Skeptic this round (the roster excludes P12). P11 carries the adversarial economic challenge and the last word before each decision; the Moderator keeps P12's smallness fork explicitly live.

Gate check on R06 / R08 (recorded so the next run does not mistake it for oversight)

Per the standing rule, a pending research file is commissioned only if a Critical decision here would otherwise sit under-grounded. It would not. Neither R06 (regulatory-primaries) nor R08 (partnerships) is commissioned. Reasoning:

  • Every pricing decision rests on accepted files: R03 (the price cluster, the value-metric framework, the two segment metrics, the specialization premium, the structural WTP and land-and-expand — accepted Medium) and R05 (the supply-bound finding, the bandwidth ceiling, the per-advisor knife-edge, the LTV:CAC — accepted Medium), plus the locked R05 catalog and the brief's price ladder.
  • R06 (regulatory-primaries) feeds the durability of the regulatory-interpretation edge (Round 3 H3), not the price of the work. Pricing uses the dual-regime crosswalk as a delivery/leverage artifact (R05 D16), not as a moat requiring R06.
  • R08 (partnerships) feeds channel economics (Round 8), not the value metric or the per-advisor margin. The perimeter's platform-leverage dependency is already carried at Medium/COI with a pilot trigger (R05 #5) — the correct cell; R08 would not change it.
  • The one genuinely unliftable input — buyer WTP / the premium a buyer will pay for the niche — is not desk-reachable at all (R03 A/#1). No pending file closes it; only the signed buyer probe does. Commissioning R06/R08 would not touch it.

Decision: proceed. No defect; no intervention entry.

The frame the room adopted first: price the engine, not the service — and price is a signal, not just a number

Before any single number, P8 and P11 forced one structural rule that governs the whole price sheet, and P1/P2/P3 accepted it. It is the pricing twin of Round 5's two-engine frame.

Working rule (adopted by all five): A line item's delivery engine sets its pricing logic. CORE-engine work is priced on the specialization premium (R03 C) — value-based, fixed-fee or hybrid-retainer, anchored on the buyer's outcome, deliberately above the commodity band, because the premium is simultaneously the margin-survival condition (R05 B) and the trust signal (R04 D12; R07 §A — buyers do not pick the cheap risk advisor). PERIMETER-engine work is priced at/near the platform floor (R02: ~$5K bundled CPA, ~60% consult-fee cut) — its margin comes from platform+junior leverage, not from rate, and it is a pipeline feeder, never a profit centre and never positioned on. A core line priced at the commodity floor fails the margin knife-edge (P11). A perimeter line priced at a premium fails the market (P1 buys the platform) and, worse, makes the commodity work the visible price and collapses the halo (R07 §D). Engine first, then price.

This rule does the same work in pricing that it did in packaging: it dissolves the apparent contradiction between "do commodity work for cash" (P1) and "never let commodity pricing set the firm's identity or starve the premium" (P11/P2). The resolution is not "price everything high" or "compete on the floor" — it is two price logics on two engines.

A second framing move, owned by P8 and seconded by P10's R07 carry: for the core, price is not only a revenue lever — it is a trust signal. R07 §A established that in professional risk advisory, cost tips the decision only ~8% of the time; trust and expertise dominate. A core price visibly above the commodity band is consistent with — and reinforces — the high-specialization position (R04 D12). Pricing the wedge cheap would not win more deals; it would undercut the signal that wins them. This is the pricing counterpart to "differentiate on trust, never on price" (R04 D12). It does not license open-ended gouging — P1's finish-line and P2's examiner-justifiability are the two hard ceilings on it — but it removes "lower the price to compete" from the table for core work.

Persona positions (strongest case each; grounded in evidence)

P8 — The Pricing & Packaging Architect: "Price on the value metric, anchor on the buyer's outcome, and engineer the expansion into the price sheet. Hours are the one thing we never sell."

The catalog is locked; my job is to put a price logic on it that captures the value the wedge creates and ladders into LTV. Four moves.

  • Commit the value metric to a ratio, not just a name. The practitioner literature converges on a 3–10× ROI band with ~5–6× as the sweet spot, and at least one named practitioner (Brodie) advocates fee ≤ ~10% of annualized client benefit [Opinion · Secondary/practitioner · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally] (Consulting Success, via R03 B). So the price is set from the buyer's value down, not from our cost up. For the Bridge (C1) the value is the blocked enterprise deal: a SOC-2/diligence gate sits in front of a six-figure+ ACV contract (brief [S17][S18]). A fixed fee of roughly 5–10% of the ARR the diligence gate is blocking lands inside the 10:1 ratio and the 3–10× band — and it is defensible to the buyer because it is stated as a fraction of what they get, not a count of our hours.
  • Sell the model the market already accepts, and the one that wins the larger deals. Value-based and project pricing dominate consulting (~26% value-based / 37% project / 21% hourly / 13% retainer), and value-based pricers land $10K+ projects at 51% vs 39% for hourly billers [Fact · Secondary · Medium · Supporting · Use with caveat] (Consulting Success 2023 survey, via R03 B). So fixed-fee-on-outcome for the land, hybrid (base + outcome anchor) for the expand, and never hourly — hourly billing forfeits the specialization premium that is the firm's only margin above the commodity floor (R05 D13 OUT #4; R03 C).
  • Price the core above the commodity band — that is where the premium lives. Specialization is the single largest fee determinant in 2026; the generalist/specialist gap is decade-wide (~$250/hr vs $600+/hr) [Fact/Opinion · Secondary/SEO · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally] (ConsultFees/ConsultingDemand 2026, via R03 C). The core's effective realized rate (fixed fee ÷ productized senior-hours) must sit firmly in the specialist zone, not the generalist one — otherwise the wedge is priced away.
  • Engineer the expansion into the price sheet, because that is where the LTV is. ~70% of PS revenue sits with existing clients, logo retention ~83–85%, mid-market NRR ~108%, specialists +38% retention [Fact · Major/Secondary · Medium · Supporting · Use with caveat] (SPI/First Page Sage/McKinsey, via R03 E). So the price sheet has explicit rungs: land (fixed-fee project) → expand (hybrid retainer entered off the land) → cross-sell (2nd framework / 2nd regime / 2nd side). The stage-scaled retainer ($8–12K → $12–22K/mo as stage/risk rises, brief [S30b]) is itself a crude value metric and a built-in expansion escalator.

Concession: the price points are mine to structure but they sit on a COI-capped Medium band until the buyer probe returns (R03 A/#1) — I commit numbers as hypotheses, not as proven WTP. And I concede to P11: a price that the firm cannot fill at achievable volume is a number on a slide. I set the value-capture logic; P11 decides whether it survives the spreadsheet at the volume the bridge core can actually supply.

P1 — The SaaS Buyer: "Quote me a fixed price for a result with a date. Don't price commodity work like it's special, and don't smuggle me into an open-ended retainer."

I am the fast channel and the near-term cash. Price me wrong and the pipeline that funds the bank motion never fills.

  • What I will pay a premium for is the deal unblocked — fast, fixed, finished. When a bank prospect's diligence packet stalls my deal (the bridge trigger, D11/D14), the thing I am buying is a six-figure+ contract that is currently frozen. Against that, a fixed $30–45K to clear it credibly is cheap — it is single-digit percent of the ACV it unfreezes, and value-based pricers win precisely because the buyer is buying the outcome [Fact · Secondary · Medium · Supporting] (Consulting Success, via R03 B). I will pay the specialist premium (R03 C) for the firm that clears the bank's diligence fastest and most credibly — if it is fixed-fee with a finish line. Quote me hours and a "maturity journey" and I buy a platform and a generalist and hope.
  • Do not price commodity work like it is the wedge. I can see the platform price for SOC-2 readiness (~$5K bundled, ~60% cheaper than legacy consulting, R02). If your perimeter readiness (P-1) is priced like your dual-regime judgment, I will not believe either price. Keep the commodity line near the floor (it is cash and a feeder, not a premium), and let the core carry the premium.
  • My objection to the retainer is to the open-ended one, and it is conditional. I refuse a subscription with no nameable deliverable. I do not refuse a hybrid retainer entered after a delivered win, priced on a recurring value I can defend to my board — keeping my diligence posture current as I add the next three FI customers is a nameable, recurring outcome (R05 D15; my carried concession). Earn it off the land; do not sell it cold.

Concession: I accept that the premium is real and justified for the wedge work — the regulated depth IS the outcome for the bridge buyer (R04 reconciliation), so I will not haggle the core to the commodity floor. My hard lines stay: (1) the lead is a fixed price with a date; (2) commodity work is priced as commodity, not dressed up; (3) the retainer is value-metered and entered off a delivered result, never open-ended and never cold.

P2 — The Regulated Buyer: "Price me on what survives the exam, and make the number one I can justify to my examiner — not a body-shop hourly rate and not a startup sprint fee."

I am the anchor end — slower, heavier, more valuable — and I am priced differently from P1 on purpose.

  • My value metric is examiner defensibility and avoided enforcement, and the stakes are quantifiable. Canadian AMPs run $10K–500K CAD, and a clean exam plus an avoided MRA are measurable outcomes [Fact · Vendor/Primary · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally] (brief [S35]; R03 B). Against that downside, an examiner-defensible TPRM/exam-prep project at $35–75K+ (brief bank TPRM band [S34]) is a small fraction of the exposure it insures — that is the value frame, and it is the right one, not an hourly count.
  • My WTP is structurally real but examiner-scrutinized. The smallest community banks spend 50–64% of their consulting budget on compliance [Fact · Major · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally] (CSBS, via R03 D) — the budget exists and falls hardest on exactly my size of institution. But the FDIC explicitly warns me off "costly and unnecessary" consultants [Opinion · Primary · Medium · Supporting · Use with caveat] (FDIC, via R03 D). So the price must be one I can justify to my examiner as defensibility I could not produce internally — pay for the dual-regime judgment that survives supervision, never for commodity hours a junior or a platform should have done. A premium on judgment is justifiable; a premium on evidence collection is not, and it is the kind of spend the FDIC tells me to cut.
  • The expand rung is worth the most and is earned, not led. The examiner-cycle retainer (C4) — ongoing program ownership tied to my exam cycle and board reporting — is the heaviest, most valuable engagement, and I enter it only after a bounded land (C3) delivered to my documentation standard (R05 D15; my carried concession). Price it in the upper retainer band ($12–25K/mo+), because it is replacing or augmenting a function whose full-time cost is a CISO at $250–565K all-in (brief [S30a][S30b]) — the fractional saving (55–80%) is my secondary justification to the board.

Concession: I accept a bounded, premium-but-justifiable front door (C3) that ladders into the heavy retainer (C4). My hard lines: the bank price is anchored on defensibility and avoided enforcement (never on hours), it is examiner-justifiable, and the visible identity and pricing stay regulated-grade — if the firm's public face is cheap SOC-2 readiness, the halo that makes my premium defensible collapses (R07 §D) and I cannot justify the spend.

P3 — The Boutique Operator: "Show me the effective senior rate after the pod takes its share. If the fixed fee divided by the senior hours doesn't clear the specialist band, the premium is a story, not a price."

I deliver this. A price is only real to me as an effective realized rate per scarce senior hour.

  • The fixed fee must clear the specialist band on senior hours, not list hours. The whole point of productization (R05 D16) is that the pod and the platform take the commodity sub-tasks, so the senior's hours on a Bridge engagement are few and high-value. If the Bridge is $30–45K fixed and productization holds the senior to, say, 40–70 hours of genuine dual-regime judgment, the effective senior realization is ~$430–1,100/hr — firmly in the $600+/hr specialist zone (R03 C), well above the $250/hr generalist floor. That is the number that has to hold. If scope creep or thin productization pushes senior hours up, the effective rate decays toward the floor and the premium evaporates — same money, worse economics.
  • Price has to protect utilization, not just margin per deal. Loaded CAC is dominated by senior selling hours (R05 D) — every hour a founder spends selling or rescoping is an hour not delivered against the 5–8/3–6 ceiling (R05 A). A fixed, productized, value-anchored price is faster to sell and faster to scope than a custom hourly bid, so the price structure itself protects the capacity ceiling. A bespoke quote on every deal is a hidden senior-hour tax.
  • The perimeter price is a leverage bet, and it is gated. P-1 at $8–18K only clears margin if the platform actually delivers the ~4–5× / ~70%-workload cut on a real book — and that is a COI-capped vendor claim whose realized leverage on a regulated-adjacent book is likely lower [Opinion/Forecast · Vendor · Medium · Supporting · Use with caveat / pilot-gated] (R05 A; R05 #5). So I price P-1 near the floor and pilot-gate it: if the leverage underdelivers, P-1's effective cost is senior QA time and the line loses money. Approved-but-gated, not approved-and-scaled.

Concession: I accept P8's premium and P2's heavy bank pricing — they are deliverable if productization holds the senior-hour cost down and the bank track is sequenced to the 3–6 deep-client end (R05 A). I am not arguing the prices down; I am insisting that the effective senior rate after pod leverage is the number the firm manages, and that every quote that inflates senior hours quietly converts a premium price into a commodity margin.

P11 — The Unit-Economics & Capacity Modeler: the hard floor (the round's adversarial economic gate)

No Skeptic sits this round; I hold the floor and the last word. I do not ask whether the prices sound fair — P1/P2/P8 argue that. I ask whether the pricing system survives the spreadsheet at the volume the firm can actually supply, and I have a veto.

  • The pricing must hold both knife-edge variables at once, and it is the only lever that sets them. R05 B is explicit: per-advisor revenue (~$540–860K) and gross margin (~45–60%) hold only on high utilization and premium pricing simultaneously; drop either and the firm collapses to the SPI generalist floor (rev/consultant $199K, project margin 35.9%, EBITDA 9.8%) [Assumption/Fact · Low/Major · Critical · gate] (R05 B; SPI via R03 E). The committed prices set the premium variable directly. So let me reconstruct the per-advisor book at these numbers and check it reproduces R05 B rather than contradicting it: a senior carrying 5–6 retainers at a $9–14K/mo blend (C2 SaaS retainers in the $8–22K band; C4 bank retainers in the $12–25K band) = $540–860K gross revenue/advisor — which is exactly the R05 B model. The committed prices are internally consistent with the accepted economics; they do not invent a new number, they price the existing one. Good — but that consistency is also the warning: the model only clears a healthy boutique margin if both variables hold, and pricing alone holds only one of them.
  • Premium pricing without volume still tips the knife-edge — this is where I bind the smallness fork. The other knife-edge variable is utilization, and utilization depends on volume, not on price. If the bridge core is too thin to fill 5–6 retainers per senior (the open T2 question — is the cross-border, both-sides, stalled-FI-deal band big enough?), then even at a perfect premium price the senior is under-filled, utilization drops below ~55%, and the margin collapses toward the generalist floor regardless of how well we priced. Premium pricing is necessary but not sufficient; it cannot rescue an under-filled book. I cannot close this — it is gated on T2 and R01's adoption/ACV data. I require it to stay live in the decision text: the per-advisor revenue is a pilot hypothesis, not a proven number, until the volume that fills the book is observed.
  • The COI cap is real and I will not let it be laundered into confidence. Every price above is a seller's rate card (R03 A); none is a measured buyer WTP. The buyer pricing probe (R03 #1) is the gate — until it returns, "the buyer pays the commodity floor and the wedge cannot command a premium" stays a live alternative, exactly as the Skeptic required at the R03 sign-off. Committing the numbers as gated hypotheses is correct; treating them as validated would be the defect.
  • The good news is the part that is not fragile. The model does not break on demand, CAC, or LTV. Loaded CAC ~$15–30K (dominated by senior selling time, R05 D) against contribution LTV ~$200–300K+ (R05 E) gives LTV:CAC ~7–20×, far above the 3:1 bar [Fact (benchmark) · Secondary · Medium · Supporting · Use] (First Page Sage, via R05 E). The pricing does not endanger that — it is supply-bound, and the pricing's job is to make each scarce senior hour earn the premium, which it does if both knife-edge variables hold.

Concession: the pricing system passes my floor, with conditions. (1) The core is priced above the commodity band and the perimeter at/near the floor (done). (2) The effective senior rate after pod leverage clears the specialist band (P3's test — built in). (3) The per-advisor revenue is carried as a pilot hypothesis gated on T2 (volume) + R03 #1 (WTP/premium) + R05 #1 (real utilization), not stated as fact. (4) The smallness fork stays open in the text, not smoothed over. Meet those and the pricing clears the floor — it is a sound system whose one unproven dependency is whether the bridge core supplies the volume to fill it.

Forced disagreement → reconciliation

The room split along two fault lines; both resolve without averaging.

Fault line 1 — P8 (value-based premium + the full expansion ladder) vs P1 (fixed, finished, never open-ended, never commodity-priced-up). This is not resolved by a compromise price. It resolves by separating the two price logics onto the two engines and the two ladder rungs: - The land is P1-shaped: fixed-fee, finish-lined, value-anchored on the outcome (deal unblocked / examiner-defensible file). P1 gets the productized result with a date and a number. - The expand is P8-shaped but inside P1's constraint: a hybrid (base + outcome anchor) retainer, entered off the delivered land, priced on a nameable recurring value — not the open-ended subscription P1 refuses (R05 D15). P8 gets the LTV ladder; P1 gets a retainer that is value-metered and earned, not cold. - The commodity perimeter (P-1) is priced at the floor, satisfying P1's "don't dress up commodity work" — it is cash and a feeder, never the premium and never the visible identity.

Fault line 2 — P8 (price for maximum value-capture and LTV) vs P11/P3 (the price only holds if the book fills at achievable utilization and the effective senior rate clears the specialist band). Reconciled by making the value metric the price-setter and the senior-hour budget the throttle: - Price is set from the buyer's value down (P8's 5–6× ROI frame) — but every package is then checked against P3's effective-senior-rate-after-pod-leverage test and P11's per-advisor book reconstruction. A price that captures value but cannot be staffed at the specialist realization, or that assumes a book the bridge core cannot fill, is flagged, not committed-as-fact. - The expansion ladder is priced in full (every rung has a number) but climbed in capacity-sequenced order (P11/D16) — the firm offers the rungs its current senior bench can deliver at target utilization. The cross-sell inventory is real LTV and rationed.

P11's last challenge before each decision (the floor's veto, standing in for the Skeptic): "Name the achievable volume that fills the senior book at these prices. You cannot — T2 is open. So commit the prices as a gated pilot hypothesis, price the wedge above the commodity band because that is the one variable pricing controls, and keep the smallness fork live: a clean price sheet can hide the fact that we have not proven the core is big enough to fill the seniors these numbers assume." Acknowledged and built in — every committed number is COI-flagged and gated on R03 #1; the per-advisor reconstruction is labelled a pilot hypothesis; the smallness fork is carried into D20 and the trigger register.

DECISIONS

D17 — The pricing MODEL: value-based fixed-fee-on-outcome for the land, hybrid (base + outcome anchor) for the expand, never hourly; core priced above the commodity band, perimeter at/near the platform floor — with committed per-package bands

The model is set by delivery engine and ladder rung, not by service name. [Decision · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally · gated on R03 #1] (R03 A/B/C; R05 B/D13; R05 D15).

The model rules: - Never hourly / never cost-plus for the core. Hourly billing forfeits the specialization premium (R03 C) and caps the firm at the generalist realization (R05 D13 OUT #4). The core sells outcomes and judgment, priced from buyer value down. - Land = fixed-fee on the outcome. Bounded, productized, finish-lined (P1). Priced as a fraction of the value it unlocks (P8's 5–6× ROI frame). - Expand = hybrid (base retainer + outcome anchor), entered off a delivered land, value-metered on a nameable recurring value (R05 D15; P1's reconciliation). - Core priced above the commodity band; perimeter priced at/near the platform floor. The premium is the margin-survival condition and the trust signal (the opening frame; R04 D12; R07 §A).

Committed price bands (all COI-capped Medium per R03 A; all gated on the buyer pricing probe R03 #1; stated as a fraction of the value frame in D18):

# Package Engine Model Committed band Anchored on
C1 The Bridge Engagement (lead/land, SaaS) CORE Fixed-fee on outcome ~$30–45K fixed (above the $15–25K commodity readiness floor) deal unblocked = ~5–10% of the blocked enterprise ARR (brief [S17][S18]; R03 B)
C2 Dual-regime SaaS retainer (expand) CORE Hybrid base + outcome anchor, stage-scaled $8–12K/mo (Series A) → $12–22K/mo (Series B/regulated) (brief [S30b]) ongoing diligence-readiness as FI customers accumulate
C3 Examiner-defensible TPRM / exam-prep (land, bank) CORE Fixed-fee project on outcome ~$35–75K+ (brief bank TPRM band [S34]) examiner defensibility + avoided AMP ($10–500K CAD, brief [S35]) + avoided MRA
C4 Bank examiner-cycle retainer (expand) CORE Hybrid base + outcome anchor, exam-cycle-aligned ~$12–25K/mo+ (upper retainer band) fractional CISO replacement (vs $250–565K all-in, brief [S30a/b]) + ongoing exam/board defensibility
C5 Cross-sell extensions (2nd framework / 2nd regime / 2nd side) CORE Project step-up and/or retainer escalation 2nd framework ~$15–35K; 2nd regime (cross-border) at a premium — no single-regime competitor holds it (R02); 2nd side = the receiving side's land price the cross-border + both-sides axes = the richest cross-sell inventory (R05 D15)
P-1 Commodity SOC 2 / ISO readiness ("readiness factory") PERIMETER Fixed-fee, near platform floor ~$8–18K (vs ~$5K platform-bundled, R02) margin from platform+junior leverage, not rate; pipeline feeder; pilot-gated (R05 #5)

The bands reproduce the R05 capacity model rather than contradict it (see D20). Each number is a hypothesis priced into a pilot, not a measured WTP (R03 #1; P11/Skeptic-position carried from R03).

D18 — The value metric, committed to a ratio: the two segment metrics expressed as fee-to-value, and the anchoring frame

The two segment-specific value metrics (R03 B; R05 D15) are now expressed as fee-to-value ratios so the price can be set from the buyer's outcome and defended in the buyer's own terms. [Decision · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally · gated on R03 #1] (R03 B; Consulting Success ROI band via R03 B).

  • SaaS (C1/C2): value metric = revenue/deal unblocked. Fee set as ~5–10% of the enterprise ARR the diligence gate is blocking (inside the 3–10× ROI / 10:1 band, R03 B). A $30–45K Bridge fee against a $300K–$1M+ blocked contract is single-digit percent — the buyer reads it as cheap insurance on a frozen deal, not as a consulting bill. The retainer (C2) value-meters on diligence-readiness maintained as FI customers accumulate.
  • Bank (C3/C4): value metric = examiner defensibility + avoided enforcement. Fee set against the quantifiable downside avoided — AMPs $10–500K CAD (brief [S35]), an avoided MRA, and (for C4) the fractional saving vs a $250–565K all-in CISO. A $35–75K project against a six-figure-plus enforcement exposure is justifiable to the examiner, which is P2's binding test (FDIC caution, R03 D).
  • The anchoring frame (governs every quote): anchor on the buyer's value, never on hours or a competitor's rate card. State the fee as a fraction of the outcome ("this unfreezes a $X deal / insures against a $Y enforcement exposure"), and let the price itself signal the specialization (above the commodity band = consistent with the wedge; R04 D12; R07 §A — cost tips only ~8%). Never lead with price; never discount the core to win on price (forfeits the asset that wins the deal, R04 D12).

D19 — Anchoring + expansion mechanics: the land→expand→cross-sell pricing ladder, the stage-escalator, and the cross-border premium

The price sheet is engineered as a ladder with explicit rungs and escalators, priced in full and climbed in capacity-sequenced order. [Decision · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally · gated on R03 #1 + T2] (R03 B/E; R05 B/E/D15).

  • Anchoring within a package: the visible anchor is the value frame (D18), with the cross-border / both-sides option presented as the premium "best" that anchors the dual-regime specialization (no single-regime competitor can offer it — R02; R04 D9). The premium option does double duty: it anchors the price and surfaces the cross-sell inventory at first contact.
  • The pricing ladder (two tracks, one logic):
  • SaaS: LAND C1 (fixed-fee $30–45K on deal-unblocked) → EXPAND C2 (hybrid retainer $8–22K/mo, stage-escalating with company stage/risk — the brief's $8–12K→$12–22K band is itself the escalator and a crude value metric, brief [S30b]) → CROSS-SELL C5 (2nd framework $15–35K; 2nd regime at the cross-border premium; 2nd side = the buyer's bank counterparty).
  • Bank: LAND C3 (fixed-fee $35–75K+ on examiner-defensibility) → EXPAND C4 (hybrid retainer $12–25K/mo+, exam-cycle-aligned) → CROSS-SELL C5 (2nd regime; the bank's vendor population as new bridge-buyer pipeline = the 2nd side flowing back).
  • The retainer pricing rule (P1's binding constraint, R05 D15): the expand rung is hybrid (base + outcome anchor), entered off a delivered land, priced on a defensible recurring value (posture maintenance / next framework / next regime). It is not priced or sold as an open-ended subscription. If the firm drifts to selling open retainers cold to hit revenue, P1's resistance returns and the fast channel sours (carried dissent → Round 7).
  • The cross-border axis is the richest priced rung and a capacity test: FFIEC↔OSFI and SaaS↔bank cross-sells are unique inventory (R02) priced at a premium, and each consumes senior hours, so the firm offers the rungs its bench can deliver at target utilization (P11; R05 D16). Specialists retain +38% and expand better than generalists (R03 E) — the ladder compounds the wedge into LTV, conditional on staffing it.

D20 — The unit-economics view: the pricing reproduces the R05 model and survives — on the joint condition of premium pricing AND achievable volume; the smallness fork stays open

The decisive pricing-economics question: does the unit economics and the capacity survive the committed prices? The answer is yes, conditionally — and the condition is precisely the open fork. [Decision · Medium → numbers Low · Critical · gate — pilot hypothesis] (R05 A/B/D/E; R03 A/C).

  • The committed prices reproduce the accepted per-advisor model, they do not contradict it. A senior carrying 5–6 retainers at a $9–14K/mo blend (C2/C4) = ~$540–860K gross revenue/advisor, against a fully-loaded senior cost of ~$260–320K (R05 B/C) → ~45–60% gross delivery margin, above the SPI generalist floor (35.9% project margin, 9.8% EBITDA). Bridge/land projects (C1/C3) add project revenue and feed the retainer ladder. This is the R05 B model priced, not a new claim. [Assumption · Low numbers / Medium framework · Critical · gate] (R05 B).
  • The pricing controls one knife-edge variable (premium) and is necessary but not sufficient for the other (utilization). Pricing the core above the commodity band is what keeps the premium variable up — pricing at the floor would collapse margin to the generalist level regardless of utilization (R05 B; R03 C). But the utilization variable depends on volume, which pricing cannot manufacture: if the bridge core is too thin to fill 5–6 retainers per senior (the open T2 smallness fork), the book under-fills, utilization drops below ~55%, and margin collapses even at a perfect premium price. Premium pricing is necessary; achievable volume is the unproven co-requirement.
  • What is NOT fragile (the supply-bound conclusion holds under the committed prices): loaded CAC ~$15–30K vs contribution LTV ~$200–300K+ → LTV:CAC ~7–20×, clearing 3:1 with room (R05 D/E). The pricing does not endanger the funnel economics — WNW remains supply-bound, not demand-/CAC-/LTV-bound (R05 E). The pricing's job is to make each scarce senior hour earn the premium, which it does at the committed bands if both knife-edge variables hold.
  • The effective-senior-rate test passes by construction (P3): at a $30–45K Bridge fee with productization holding the senior to ~40–70 judgment-hours, the effective senior realization is ~$430–1,100/hr — in the specialist band (R03 C), well above the $250/hr generalist floor. The premium is real provided productization holds senior-hours down; scope creep that inflates senior hours silently converts the premium price into a commodity margin (P3's standing warning → Round 9 instrumentation).
  • Verdict (P11, the floor): the pricing system clears the economic floor as a sound, internally-consistent model whose one unproven dependency is whether the bridge core supplies the volume to fill the senior books the per-advisor numbers assume. The per-advisor revenue/margin is therefore committed as a pilot hypothesis, gated on T2 (volume / bridge-core size), R03 #1 (buyer WTP / the premium over single-regime/platform alternatives), and R05 #1 (WNW's real utilization and senior-hours-per-engagement). The double-edged-smallness fork stays OPEN — not smoothed over by a clean price sheet.

Remaining dissent (carried, not resolved)

  • P11/P12 — the double-edged-smallness / bridge-core-size fork is OPEN (the round's central unresolved tension). The committed prices reproduce a per-advisor model that assumes 5–6 fillable senior retainers; whether the cross-border, both-sides, stalled-FI-deal band is big enough to fill them is unproven (T2 + R01 adoption/ACV). Premium pricing cannot rescue an under-filled book. → Round 7 (does the GTM motion generate the volume to fill the books?), Round 9 (instrument utilization and pipeline-to-core from day one), and the synthesis trigger register.
  • All committed price points sit on a COI-capped Medium band. Every number in D17/D18/D19 is a seller's rate card (R03 A), not a measured buyer WTP. "The buyer pays the commodity floor and the wedge cannot command a premium" stays a live alternative until the buyer pricing probe (R03 #1) returns. → Round 7 (price into a pilot; instrument realized ACV vs list) and the synthesis as the gate trigger.
  • P1 — the retainer reconciliation holds only for value-metered, earned-into renewals. If revenue pressure pushes the firm to sell open-ended retainers cold, P1's resistance returns and the fast channel sours. → Round 7 (the sell motion must land the productized fixed-fee front door first).
  • P2 — the bank price must stay examiner-justifiable and the visible identity regulated-grade. If the commodity perimeter (P-1) becomes the firm's visible price, the halo that makes the bank premium defensible collapses (R07 §D), and P2 can no longer justify the spend to the examiner (FDIC caution). → Round 7/Round 8 (keep P-1 a quiet capability, never the positioned price).
  • P3 — the premium is real only while productization holds senior-hours down. Scope creep converts a premium price into a commodity margin without changing the headline number. → Round 9 (instrument senior-hours-per-engagement and effective realized rate).
  • P3/P11 — the perimeter price (P-1) is a leverage bet, pilot-gated. The ~$8–18K floor pricing only clears margin if the platform delivers ~4–5× on a real regulated-adjacent book; the realized leverage is a COI-capped vendor claim (R05 #5). Approved-but-gated. → Round 8 (platform partner-vs-threat) and Round 9 (the perimeter-leverage pilot).

Validation triggers (Round 6 — no new triggers; existing ones re-bound to the committed prices)

Round 6 introduces no new validation triggers — the honest outcome, since pricing is a structure-and-numbers decision built on already-accepted research and an already-signed COI cap. It re-binds the existing triggers to the committed numbers:

  • R03 #1 — buyer WTP / the premium over single-regime/platform alternatives (Critical × Low — the gate residual). Now binds every committed price point in D17/D18/D19. The numbers are hypotheses priced into a pilot until the buyer probe returns. NEEDS EXTERNAL RETRIEVAL: buyer pricing probe — present the committed bands to 8–12 target buyers per segment; capture acceptable price, the realized premium (if any) over a single-regime specialist or platform, and the value-frame resonance. (Same probe R03 signed with Skeptic sign-off.)
  • T2 — bridge-core size / the smallness fork (Critical × Low). Now binds D20 directly: is the core big enough to fill the senior books the committed per-advisor revenue assumes? Premium pricing cannot rescue under-filled utilization. Closed by R01's adoption/ACV data + the buyer-probe family.
  • R05 #1 — WNW's real utilization, senior-hours-per-engagement, ACV, retention, NRR (Critical × Low). Binds the per-advisor reconstruction in D20 and the effective-senior-rate test (P3). Closed by the WNW internal data pull (one pull closes the R03 #2 renewal trigger too).
  • R05 #5 — realized platform leverage on a regulated book (Medium, pilot-gated). Binds the P-1 floor pricing in D17. Closed by the delivery pilot.

All sit in the same buyer-probe / internal-data family flagged since R01–R05: one buyer pricing engagement plus one WNW internal data pull can close the load-bearing set together.

Moderator's quality note (intervention check)

No defect logged. Personas stayed distinct and argued from their own priors: P8 set price from buyer value down, committed the value metric to a fee-to-value ratio, and engineered the expansion ladder, conceding the points sit on a COI-capped band and the ladder is capacity-rationed; P1 pressed the fixed-fee/finish-line/anti-open-retainer/anti-commodity-markup case and conceded only the value-metered, earned-into renewal and the justified wedge premium; P2 held the examiner-defensibility value metric and the examiner-justifiability test, conceding only a bounded premium front door that ladders into the heavy retainer; P3 grounded every price in the effective senior rate after pod leverage and the founder-selling-time capacity tax; P11 held the economic floor, reconstructed the per-advisor book at the committed prices (confirming they reproduce — not contradict — the R05 model), and bound the smallness fork as the one unproven dependency, taking the last challenge before each decision. Genuine disagreement was forced along the two real fault lines (P8-value-capture vs P1-fixed-and-finished; P8-LTV-ladder vs P11/P3-staffable-at-achievable-volume) and reconciled by structure, not by averaging — two price logics on two engines, value-metric as the price-setter, and the senior-hour budget as the throttle.

With no Skeptic on this roster, P11 carried the adversarial economic role and the veto (the "name the achievable volume that fills the book" challenge), and the Moderator kept P12's two carried tensions explicitly live and re-bound: the double-edged-smallness / bridge-core-size fork (gated T2, carried into D20 and the dissent register as the round's central unresolved tension) and the perimeter-starves-the-core / leverage risk (P-1 priced at the floor and pilot-gated on R05 #5). Every committed price is grounded in an accepted research file (R03/R05) at its stated confidence and explicitly flagged COI-capped Medium and gated on the signed buyer pricing probe (R03 #1) — committing numbers inside an accepted-but-COI-capped band, clearly labelled and gated, is the standard's prescribed treatment for a Critical×Medium input, not a breach. The pricing points were the round's whole assignment, and they were committed as gated hypotheses, not waved through as proven WTP. Round 7 — GTM motion and channels

The question on the table

Rounds 1–6 built the what and the who: two ICPs bridged not merged (R01), the bridge-sequencing thesis (R02), the holdable white space (R03), the positioning and wedge (R04), the two-engine catalog with the Bridge Engagement as the lead package (R05), and the committed pricing that reproduces the per-advisor economics (R06). Round 7 builds the how does the next client actually arrive — and it inherits one unanswered question that R06 handed up explicitly:

R06 D20 (the open smallness fork): the whole pricing model survives only if the bridge core supplies enough VOLUME to fill the senior books the per-advisor numbers assume. Premium pricing controls one knife-edge variable; utilization is the other, and utilization is volume-dependent. Premium pricing cannot rescue an under-filled book. So Round 7's central pressure test is: does the GTM motion generate the volume to fill the books?

That is why the Skeptic is seated this round. The motion is not just "how do we sell" — it is the test of whether the wedge is wide enough to be a business.

The round does not relitigate settled work. The positioning is fixed (R04: the dual-regime vendor-diligence & examiner-defensibility specialist, entered via the bridge buyer at the bridge trigger). The catalog is fixed (R05: C1 Bridge Engagement / C2 SaaS retainer / C3 examiner-defensible TPRM+exam-prep / C4 bank exam-cycle retainer / C5 cross-sell / P-1 perimeter). The prices are fixed (R06: the land is a productized fixed-fee front door — C1 $30–45K, C3 $35–75K+ — and the retainer is entered off a delivered land, never sold cold). Round 7 takes those as the offers it originates on and decides the motion and the channels that put them in front of the right buyer.

The binding constraints (carried in the ledger's next_unit — the motion may not break these)

  1. The engine is NOT a referral motion. Referrals are the cheapest pipeline WNW has but are structurally declining — referral-as-search-method down ~15% over five years, referral-making down ~5% [Fact · Major · Medium · Critical] (Hinge, via R04 A). A referral-only motion is a depreciating asset. The documented scale mechanism is expertise-led origination that manufactures referability (R04 C). But the thought-leadership→conversion link is a Medium inference on a High belief base (R04 B) — treat it as a hypothesis to instrument from day one, not a proven funnel.
  2. The two segments do NOT share a channel (R04 E). SaaS = faster, digital, platform-mediated (compounds fast, commoditizes fast). Bank = slower, peer/association-gated, trust-heavy (compounds slow, defends well). This channel asymmetry is an independent reason for different launch timing — it binds R02 D6.
  3. R02 D6 sequencing BINDS. Dominate the bridge: lead the bridge subset via the SaaS-side fast channel (ACCESS); earn the regulated identity through delivery (the bridge manufactures examiner-facing references = the bank channel's entry ticket); compound into bank-direct (QUALITY). US-first for volume; Canada/OSFI differentiation from day 1 + a second wave on the Streamlined cohort (P7's carried dissent: the Canadian tailwind is freshest now — don't forfeit first-mover).
  4. Cold outbound is corrected to 3–6% reply, not the brief's "<2%" (R04 D) — low-yield and worsening; not the engine, at most a precision-targeted supplement.
  5. Loaded CAC IS senior selling time (R05 D). Origination that does not consume senior hours linearly (expertise-led + referral/partner) is doubly justified — it protects the capacity ceiling, not just the budget. WNW is supply-bound; the founder's selling time and the founder's delivery time are the same scarce hours.
  6. The motion must LAND the productized fixed-fee front door FIRST (R06; P1): land C1/C3, then enter the hybrid retainer off the delivered land — or the retainer reconciliation sours.

Gate check on R08 / R06-regulatory (recorded so the next run does not mistake it for oversight)

Per the standing rule, a pending file is commissioned only if a Critical decision here would otherwise sit under-grounded. It would not.

  • R08 (partnerships) is NOT commissioned for this round. Round 7 decides that partner/borrowed-pipeline is a primary origination channel and why (the bridge-trigger detection problem below makes it structurally necessary, not just faster). The exchange that makes each specific partner type send deals — the auditor/C3PAO co-sell terms, the CPA reciprocal, the insurer/broker mechanic, the GRC-platform partner-program economics and the double-edge management — is Round 8's decision and is explicitly deferred to it. R04 F already establishes at Medium that these are origination channels, not just credibility props, which is all Round 7's channel-plan decision needs. The partner plan sits on accepted R04 F + R07 §B (operator pedigree / same-industry references as trust assets); the partner terms wait for R08.
  • R06-regulatory is NOT commissioned. The GTM motion rests on channel and trust evidence (R04, R07), not on regulatory-interpretation durability. No GTM decision here sits solely on a pending file.
  • The one genuinely unliftable input — WNW's own origination mix for its last ~10–20 clients (R04 trigger #1) — is not desk-reachable; no pending file closes it. It is the single input that would lift the channel premise from Medium to High, and it is the same buyer-probe / internal-pull family already signed across R01–R05. It binds the volume question (D24) but does not block the motion, which clears Medium without it (R04's own gate finding).

Decision: proceed. No defect; no intervention entry.

The frame the room adopted first: separate the ENGINE that originates from the ASSET that converts — and budget origination in senior-hours, not dollars

Before any channel, P5 and P3 forced one structural rule, and P9/P1/P2 accepted it. It is the GTM twin of the two-engine frame that has governed packaging (R05) and pricing (R06).

Working rule (adopted by all five): Distinguish the origination engine (what creates a new qualified conversation) from the conversion asset (what turns it into a signed land), and budget the engine in senior-hours consumed, not dollars spent.

Origination has three reachable engines, ranked by cost-in-senior-hours: (1) borrowed pipeline (partner/peer/association pre-endorsement — near-zero senior hours per lead, already trusts the buyer); (2) owned expertise-led origination (published dual-regime judgment that compounds and is found by the 95%-not-in-market buyer — front-loaded senior hours to create the asset, then near-zero marginal hours to distribute it); (3) warm referral (cheapest of all but finite, declining, and not steerable — a harvest, not an engine you can turn up). Cold outbound is a supplement at 3–6%, never an engine.

The conversion asset is the productized front door (C1/C3, R05/R06) plus the proof library the firm has earned. The land closes on trust (R07 §A: trust ≈ cost/quality, cost tips only ~8%), so the conversion asset is proof, not a pitch — peer referral > same-industry reference > operator pedigree > third-party validation (R07 §B).

This rule does three things at once. It dissolves the P5-vs-P9 turf fight before it starts — borrowed pipeline and owned origination are not competing strategies but different-cost engines feeding the same front door, and the right question is sequence and senior-hour budget, not "which one." It operationalizes R05's "loaded CAC = senior hours" — every channel is scored by the senior-hours it burns per qualified conversation, which is the binding currency, because the founder's selling hours are stolen from the founder's delivery hours (P3). And it enforces R04 D12 / R06's price-is-a-signal at the channel layer: the motion sells proof and being-found-pre-trusted, never being-pitched-on-price, because the buyer runs ~70–80% of the journey before contact and meets all suppliers for only ~17% of it (R04 D).

Persona positions (strongest case each; grounded in evidence)

P5 — The GTM & Demand Engine Lead: "Borrowed pipeline buys the first year; the OWNED expertise engine is the only thing that makes the next ten clients repeatable. Build it from day one or you rent your pipeline forever."

Where do the next ten clients come from, and what makes that source repeat? That is the only GTM question that matters, and a referral motion fails it on the evidence: referrals are not just finite, they are secularly declining as a discovery channel (Hinge, R04 A). A firm whose only engine is "our network refers us" is running on a depreciating asset. So the engine has to be something WNW owns and can turn up.

  • The owned engine is expertise-led origination, and it is the documented substitute for the eroding referral channel. Hinge's finding is not "publish for vanity" — it is that high-growth professional-services firms substitute Visible Expertise for the referral channel that is failing them, and that 82% of buyers check the firm's website when evaluating a provider [Fact · Major · Medium · Critical · Build provisionally] (Hinge, R04 B). The Edelman–LinkedIn primary is the strongest source we have: 73% of decision-makers trust thought-leadership content over marketing as a basis for judging competence; ~90% are more receptive to outreach from a firm that consistently publishes it; 75% say a piece of TL moved them to consider something new [Opinion (stated belief) · Major · High · Critical · Build] (Edelman-LinkedIn 2024, R04 B). And the structural reason it compounds where outbound does not: ~95% of buyers are not in-market at any given moment (R04 B) — published expertise captures them before the buying cycle; cold outbound interrupts the 5% and annoys the 95%.
  • Specialization is the channel multiplier — this is why a boutique CAN out-publish in a sliver even though it can't in a broad market. The narrower the niche, the cheaper it is to own its mindshare (R04 C). WNW is not trying to be the visible expert in "cybersecurity" — it is trying to be the visible expert in dual-regime vendor-diligence and examiner-defensibility for the software-to-bank trust chain. That is narrow enough that a two-to-three-person firm can plausibly dominate the search results, the conference stages, and the peer conversations for it. P12 will say "content is vanity"; my answer is content is vanity in a broad market and a moat in a narrow one — and our market is narrow by construction (R04 C; R02 wedge).
  • The flywheel is "manufactured referability," and the Bridge Engagement is the flywheel's hub. Here is the engine, concretely: publish the dual-regime judgment → get found by the 95%-not-in-market bridge buyer AND give the existing network something concrete to refer (R04 C) → land a Bridge Engagement (C1) → the engagement produces a dual artifact (R05 D14): the SaaS deal-unblock story and the examiner-legible reference → that reference is the next buyer's peer-referral proof (R07 §B: 71% find firms by asking a person) → which feeds both the content (case material) and the referral channel (referable proof). The flywheel manufactures the referrals that the market is no longer giving away for free.
  • Concession to P9: the owned engine is slow to compound — it front-loads senior hours and converts on a lag (R04 B: receptiveness is High but content-to-conversion is a Medium inference, not a measured rate). In year one it will not fill the books on its own. So I do not claim the owned engine is the day-one engine. I claim it must be started day one because it takes a year-plus to compound, and the firm that delays it is renting its pipeline from P9's partners forever — with no owned asset and no pricing power when the partner relationship reprices. Borrow now; own in parallel.
  • Concession to P3 and P12: the content thesis is a hypothesis to instrument, not a proven funnel (R04 trigger #2). I will instrument inbound-attributed pipeline and content-influenced win rate from launch, and if the niche-content-to-conversion rate does not materialize, the engine reweights toward partner + referral and I eat the result. I am not allowed to assume the funnel; I am required to measure it.

P9 — The Partnerships & Ecosystem Builder: "You can't out-publish your way to the first year, and worse — you can't even SEE the bridge trigger without a partner sitting at it. Borrowed pipeline isn't the faster path; for our wedge it's the ONLY path to the trigger in time."

P5's flywheel is real but it spins up too slowly to pay the rent, and there is a deeper structural problem P5 hasn't named. Let me make the strongest case for partner-led origination, including the part that's genuinely novel.

  • Partners already have the buyer's trust — that is the whole game in risk advisory. The decision closes on trust, and the shortest trust path is peer referral and same-industry reference (R07 §B: 71% of buyers find firms by asking a person; same-industry references carry +37% credibility; ~79% of CISOs trust peers most). A partner who already sits next to the buyer — the auditor running the SOC 2, the GRC platform routing readiness work, the cyber-insurer's broker, the FS-ISAC peer-CISO group — pre-endorses WNW, which collapses the trust leap that content takes a year to build. Borrowed pipeline is near-zero senior-hours per lead (the partner does the prospecting and the vouching), which under R05's supply-bound logic is the single most capacity-efficient origination there is.
  • The novel point — the bridge trigger is HIGH-INTENT but LOW-OBSERVABILITY, and only a partner can catch it in time. R02/R04 say the bridge trigger (a SaaS vendor's FI deal stalls on third-party security diligence) is the single highest-intent event touching both ICPs. True — but you cannot market to that moment from the outside. There is no list of "SaaS vendors whose bank deal stalled last Tuesday." The event is visible to exactly four parties: the SaaS vendor (who is panicking and Googling — that's P5's inbound capture, real but a coin-flip on timing), the FI's vendor-risk team, the auditor doing the diligence, and the GRC platform mediating it. Three of the four are partners. So partners are not merely the faster channel for the bridge — they are the detection mechanism for the trigger the entire strategy is built on. Without a partner at the diligence table, WNW only catches the bridge buyer if the buyer happens to find the content at the right week. [Opinion/Moderator-grade argument · grounded in R04 E/F + R02 bridge-trigger]
  • The bank side proves the point harder. On the bank segment, cold outbound is weakest and referrals erode fastest; R04 F's explicit finding is that partner and peer-network endorsement is the highest-yield reachable channel for banks — FS-ISAC/CB-ISAC peer-CISO groups, ABA/ICBA/state associations, CCUA/CBA in Canada. And R04 E corrects the tempting error: examiners do NOT refer vendors. The exam finding creates demand; the origination comes from the peer who survived their own exam and says "use these people." That peer reference is exactly what the Bridge Engagement manufactures (R05 D14) — which is why the bank channel is earned, not bought.
  • Concession to P5: borrowed pipeline has a tax I won't hide. It is not owned — the partner can reprice, change programs, or build the capability in-house (the GRC-platform double edge from R02: Vanta/Drata partner programs are co-sell and an up-market competitor). A firm that is 100% partner-originated has no pricing power and no defensibility at the channel layer. So I concede P5's core point: partner pipeline is the day-one engine precisely because it's fast and capacity-cheap, but it must be converted into owned assets — every partner-originated land must produce a reference and a content artifact (the flywheel) so that by year two the firm is not a pipeline tenant. Borrow to start; bank the proceeds as owned proof.
  • Concession to P3: partner channels are not free of senior hours either — they take senior relationship time to establish and a credible operator to make the partner comfortable vouching (R07 §B-3: operator pedigree is itself the trust signal that makes a partner willing to refer). That's a real senior-hour cost; it's just front-loaded into a few relationships rather than spread across hundreds of cold touches.

P3 — The Boutique Operator: "Every one of these channels spends the SAME scarce hours — the founders'. The motion isn't 'pick a channel,' it's 'which channels originate WITHOUT stealing the founders' delivery time,' because in year one selling time IS delivery time."

I have run this firm. Here is the reality the demand-gen optimism skips: in a two-to-three-senior boutique, the people who sell are the people who deliver, and they are the binding constraint (R05: supply-bound; loaded CAC = senior selling time). So I score every channel by one number — senior-hours consumed per qualified conversation — because every hour the founder spends originating is an hour not spent delivering a billable engagement, and the founder cannot do both at once.

  • The founder-led phase is unavoidable and it is a trap if you don't plan its exit. Founders close the first clients — their pedigree is the trust asset (R07 §B-3), and no junior can sell examiner-defensibility to a CISO in year one. But founder-led selling is the thing that caps the firm: the founder is simultaneously the best closer and the scarcest deliverer. The whole point of the motion is to lift origination off the founder's senior hours as fast as possible — and the rank order for doing that is exactly P5/P9's engines scored by senior-hour cost: warm referral (≈0 founder hours, but finite) → partner pre-endorsement (low founder hours, P9) → owned content distribution (≈0 marginal founder hours once the asset exists, though the asset costs founder hours to create, P5) → cold outbound (high hours, low yield — cut it as an engine).
  • What actually lifts origination off the senior layer: the productized front door + a non-senior funnel motion. R06 committed C1/C3 as productized, fixed-scope, fixed-fee front doors. Productization is what lets a non-founder BD person plus a junior pod run the top and middle of the funnel — qualification, scoping, scheduling, proposal — reserving the founder's hours for the trust moment (the diagnostic conversation and the close) and the delivery. Without productization, every deal is a custom sale only the founder can run, and the firm never scales past the founders' calendar. The front door is the scalability mechanism, not just the pricing mechanism.
  • The honest sequencing constraint. You cannot build P5's owned content engine and establish P9's partner relationships and deliver the early engagements with three founders' worth of hours. Something waits. My ranking: partner + warm referral first (lowest senior-hour cost per land, fastest cash, fills the early book), owned content started in parallel but at a sustainable cadence (one deep wedge artifact a month beats a launch-week blitz that then dies — the asset compounds only if it's consistent, R04 B), cold outbound never as a primary engine. The founders' content time is carved out deliberately (e.g. a fixed weekly block) and protected from delivery, or it will always lose to the urgent billable work and the owned engine will never get built — which is how most boutiques end up permanent pipeline tenants.
  • Where I hold the floor: the motion is only real if it pencils at the senior-hours the firm actually has. A channel plan that needs more founder selling-hours than exist after delivery is a slide, not a plan. I will sign a motion that (a) starts on capacity-cheap borrowed/referral pipeline, (b) protects a fixed founder block to build the owned asset, and (c) uses the productized front door to push the funnel onto non-senior staff — and I will veto any version that assumes the founders can originate at scale and deliver at target utilization simultaneously.

P1 — The SaaS Buyer: "I move fast and I buy digitally. I'll find you when my deal stalls — IF you're findable and you have a finish-lined offer I can say yes to that afternoon. Don't make me sit through a relationship-building cycle when I have a contract waiting."

I'm the bridge buyer on the SaaS side, and my channel is not the bank's. When my enterprise/FI deal stalls on a security-diligence gate, I am in acute pain now — I have ARR waiting on the other side of that gate (brief [S17][S18]). Here's how you actually reach me.

  • I research independently and decide most of it before I ever contact you. I run ~70–80% of the buying journey rep-free (R04 D); I'll Google "SOC 2 for selling into banks," "bank vendor security questionnaire help," "FFIEC vendor diligence consultant," I'll ask my founder Slack and my investor's platform team "who did you use," and I'll check your website (82% of buyers do — R04 B). So the channels that win me are: being findable for the exact pain phrase (P5's wedge-scoped content, but written to my frame — "pass the bank's security diligence," not "dual-regime examiner-defensibility," which is a phrase I don't use yet — this is exactly R04's T7 frame-recognition trigger), a warm intro from a peer founder or my investor/accelerator (R04 E: investors treat SOC 2 as a maturity gate; ~70% of VCs prefer SOC 2-compliant startups), and a partner who's already in my diligence loop (P9's point — my auditor or the GRC platform routing me).
  • Give me a finish-lined offer or I bounce. I refuse open-ended retainers and consulting theater (my standing position). The Bridge Engagement is exactly right because it's productized, fixed-fee, time-bounded, and priced on the deal it unblocks (R05 D14 / R06: C1 $30–45K as ~5–10% of the blocked ARR). That is something I can approve this afternoon against a six-figure contract I'm trying to save. The motion must lead with that offer, not with "let's scope a program." Lead with the program and you lose me to a faster competitor or to my just-buying-the-platform.
  • My one warning to the firm (and I know R04/R02 flagged it): don't let the regulated-anchor positioning make you slow and heavy in how you reach me. P2's world is slow; mine isn't. If the motion is built for P2's pace, it'll miss my window. The reconciliation (R02 D6) says the SaaS-fast channel is the access point precisely because I move fast — so the fast motion has to actually be fast: findable, finish-lined, same-week. Instrument whether the regulated anchor suppresses my origination speed (R04 carry: the F8/Medium tension) — if "examiner-defensible" messaging reads as enterprise-heavy and scares off the Series A founder, you've broken your own access channel.

P2 — The Regulated Buyer: "I will not be rushed, and I should not be. I buy on documented proof, peer endorsement, and a face I've seen at my association — none of which you can accelerate with a content blitz. Earn my channel; you can't buy it."

I'm the bank CISO/CRO. My channel is the opposite of P1's, and trying to run P1's fast-digital motion at me will fail — worse, it will signal that you don't understand my world, which is itself disqualifying.

  • I buy through peers and associations, on documented proof — over months. I find providers by asking peers (R07 §B: 71%; ~79% of CISOs trust peers most), in venues you can't shortcut: FS-ISAC monthly CISO peer meetings, CB-ISAC for the smaller banks, ABA/ICBA/state-association councils and conferences (R04 E). What moves me is a same-industry reference — another bank my size that survived its exam with your help (R07 §B: +37% credibility; this is the bank-channel entry ticket) — and an operator pedigree that tells me you've done this in a regulated shop (R07 §B-3). My due diligence is mandated and documented (SR 24-2 interagency TPRM guidance, R07 §C) — I have to be able to defend the choice to an examiner. None of that compresses to a quarter.
  • This is why my channel is EARNED, not bought — and why it comes second. I accept R02 D6's sequence: WNW reaches me after it has delivered bridge engagements that manufactured the examiner-facing references and the same-industry proof that I require (R05 D14). A firm with no bank references and no examiner-legible track record has nothing to say to me on day one — correctly, the motion doesn't try. It earns the proof on the SaaS-fast side first, then walks into my association with a reference I recognize. What I add: the bank motion's "channel" is really a proof-and-presence motion — show up consistently at the right associations (speaking/sponsoring = visible expertise to a regulated audience, R04 E), accumulate same-industry references, and let the peer network carry you. That takes a year-plus of presence before it pays, which is fine — it also defends far better once established (R04 E: bank channel compounds slow, defends well).
  • One thing I will not concede: do not let the firm's visible identity become the commodity SaaS work it does for cash. If what my peers see is "the SOC 2 shop," the trust does not transfer up to me — the halo degrades under commercial load (R07 §D; R04/R02 P2-condition). The motion must keep the visible WNW regulated and judgment-led even while the perimeter engine quietly does commodity work for margin (R05 D13 / R04 D10). My channel runs on the regulated identity; protect it.

The Skeptic's challenges (P12 — last word before each decision)

P12 was seated specifically to pressure two things: the content thesis and the smallness fork. It steelmanned both to their strongest form before each decision was recorded.

On the motion (vs D21): "The 'expertise-led origination engine' is the consultant's favorite vanity project. Here is the strongest case that it's hot air: the content-to-client conversion rate for a firm like WNW is unmeasured — R04 itself grades it a Medium inference on a High belief base (R04 B, trigger #2). You are betting the firm's scalability on a funnel nobody has shown converts in this niche. Strip the optimism and what's actually left is what every boutique actually runs on: warm referral and a couple of partner relationships. Why isn't the honest motion just 'P9's borrowed pipeline plus referrals, and the content is a website that exists so we don't look dead'?"

The room's answer, which P12 accepted with a binding condition: the distinction P12 is pressing is real and is exactly why the motion does not bet on content as the year-one engine. The motion is borrowed-pipeline-plus-referral first (P9/P3 carried that). But "content is vanity" is true only in a broad market — R04 C establishes that specialization is a channel multiplier, and the wedge is narrow by construction, so owning its mindshare is cheap in a way that owning "cybersecurity" mindshare is not. The content thesis survives only because it is bound to the narrow wedge and instrumented from day one — if the niche-content-to-pipeline rate doesn't materialize (R04 trigger #2), the engine reweights to partner+referral and the firm has lost a fixed founder time-block, not the business. P12's condition, recorded: content is a metered experiment scoped to the wedge, not a faith-based budget line; the day-one engine is borrowed + referral, and any claim that content "fills the funnel" stays a hypothesis with a kill-criterion until the instrumentation returns.

On the channel plan (vs D22): "You've built the entire strategy on a trigger you just admitted you can't observe (P9's own point). If three of the four parties who can see the bridge trigger are partners, then your 'channel plan' is really 'hope the partners cooperate,' and you have no leverage over them on day one — you're the small new firm asking the auditor to send you their client. What's the case that this channel exists at all before you have references the partners respect?"

Accepted as a sharpening, not a refutation: it's correct that partner cooperation is not free on day one and that the firm has thin leverage early — which is precisely why the motion makes manufactured referability the flywheel hub (the Bridge Engagement produces the reference that earns the next partner's confidence) and why the partner terms are deferred to R08 as a Critical decision rather than waved through here. The honest version recorded in D22: the bridge-trigger detection channel is real but thin at launch and thickens only as references accumulate — so the early funnel cannot rely on it alone; the founder's own network and inbound-from-acute-pain (P1) carry the first lands, which then buy the partner relationships. P12's residual flagged: partner-originated pipeline yield is unmeasured (R04 #4) — bound to R08 and to the origination audit; do not assume partners deliver volume until instrumented.

On the GTM economics / the smallness fork (vs D24): "This is the one that matters and you keep deferring it. R06 handed you a direct question: does the motion generate the volume to fill the senior books the per-advisor pricing assumes? You have built an elegant motion and you still cannot answer it, because the bridge-subset size (T2) and the niche demand (T1) are unmeasured. Premium pricing cannot rescue an under-filled book (R06 D20). What if the wedge is simply too narrow to fill even two senior calendars — what would have to be true for you to be wrong, and are you measuring it?"

The room conceded this cannot be closed on desk evidence and recorded it honestly in D24 (below) rather than smoothing it. What can be said: the motion is architected to maximize volume from a narrow wedge — multi-engine (not single-channel-dependent), anchored on the highest-intent event (the bridge trigger), and capacity-cheap so the founders can run more pipeline per available hour. What cannot be said is that the volume is proven. The smallness fork stays OPEN, carried as T2, with an explicit instrumentation-and-fallback structure. P12's last word, recorded verbatim in spirit: "Then the motion is sound conditional on a number you don't have. Say so in the plan, instrument it from week one, and define what under-fill triggers a pivot — don't let an elegant funnel diagram stand in for the volume you haven't measured." Agreed.

Decisions

D21 — The sales motion: a three-phase evolution from founder-led to scalable, with origination ranked by senior-hours consumed

The motion is expertise-anchored but borrowed-pipeline-led at launch, evolving in three phases. The organizing principle (the frame above): originate through the lowest-senior-hour engine available, lift origination off the founder layer as fast as the productized front door allows, and build the owned asset in parallel so the firm is not a permanent pipeline tenant.

  • Phase 1 — Founder-led, borrowed-pipeline-led (≈ months 0–12). The founders sell; their pedigree is the trust asset (R07 §B-3). The day-one engines, ranked by senior-hour cost: (1) warm referral from the founders' existing network (≈0 hours, finite — harvest it but don't depend on it); (2) partner/peer pre-endorsement (P9 — auditors, GRC-platform routing, investor/accelerator nodes on the SaaS side; the founders' regulated relationships on the bank side); (3) inbound from acute pain (P1 — the bridge buyer Googling the stall, captured by wedge-scoped content). Cold outbound is a precision supplement only (3–6%, R04 D), never an engine. The land is always the productized fixed-fee front door (C1 $30–45K / C3 $35–75K+, R06); the retainer is entered off the delivered land (R06; P1). A protected founder time-block is carved out for content creation from week one (P3) — the owned asset takes a year+ to compound, so delay is fatal to it.
  • Phase 2 — De-risking the founder dependency (≈ months 12–24). A non-founder BD/qualification capability plus a junior pod runs the top and middle of the productized funnel (P3), reserving founder hours for the trust moment and the close. The owned expertise engine begins to show measurable inbound (or it doesn't — instrumented per R04 #2). The manufactured-referability flywheel (D23) is now turning: early Bridge Engagements have produced references that feed both content and the partner/peer channels. The bank channel begins to open as same-industry references accumulate (P2; R02 D6 QUALITY phase).
  • Phase 3 — Scalable, multi-engine (≈ month 24+). Origination runs on three compounding owned/semi-owned engines (referral-manufactured-by-delivery, owned wedge content, established partner relationships) plus the productized front door run substantially by non-founder staff. The founder layer is reserved for senior delivery, the highest-trust closes, and the cross-border/both-sides cross-sell rungs (C5) that only senior judgment can sell. This is the state in which the per-advisor economics (R06 D20) can actually hold — if the volume is there (D24).

Risk weight: the structure is built on accepted R04 (channel premise + per-segment map, Medium), R05 (supply-bound, loaded CAC = senior hours, Medium), R07 (proof hierarchy, Medium). The content-engine leg is a Critical × Medium inference (R04 B/#2) — built provisionally, instrumented from day one with a kill-criterion. Skeptic: accepted with the binding condition that content is a metered experiment, not a faith line; the day-one engine is borrowed + referral.

D22 — The channel plan: two non-shared channels on the R02-D6 sequence, joined by the bridge-trigger detection problem

The two segments get two distinct channel motions on different timing, exactly as R04 E and R02 D6 require. They are joined at one point: the bridge trigger, which is high-intent but low-observability.

  • SaaS channel (fast, digital, platform-mediated — the ACCESS channel, leads):
  • Findability for the buyer's own frame (P1; R04 D): wedge-scoped content and search presence written to "pass the bank's security diligence / survive the vendor questionnaire," not to the internal Venn label (this is R04's T7 frame-recognition trigger — copy must be tested on real buyers before it's committed).
  • Investor/accelerator and founder-peer nodes (R04 E): VC platform teams and accelerators as referral nodes (SOC 2 = maturity gate; ~70% of VCs prefer SOC 2-compliant startups); founder/operator peer communities where "who did you use" gets asked.
  • The GRC-platform partner & auditor ecosystem (R04 E/F): co-sell routing from Vanta/Drata/Secureframe/Sprinto partner programs and platform-aligned auditors — the concrete SaaS channel, and the double edge (channel + up-market competitor) whose terms are R08's decision.
  • Bank channel (slow, peer/association-gated, trust-heavy — the QUALITY channel, earned into second):
  • Peer-CISO / information-sharing communities (R04 E): FS-ISAC monthly CISO peer meetings, CB-ISAC for smaller community banks — the confidential venues where bank references circulate.
  • Banking associations & conferences (R04 E): ABA, ICBA, state associations (US); CBA, CCUA (Canada) — speaking/sponsoring = visible expertise to a regulated audience.
  • Same-industry references as the entry ticket (R07 §B; P2): the bank channel does not open until the bridge has manufactured examiner-legible, bank-recognizable references. Structural correction held on the record: examiners/regulators do not refer vendors (R04 E) — exam findings drive demand, the peer drives origination.
  • The bridge-trigger detection problem (the round's key analytic addition): the bridge trigger (a SaaS vendor's FI deal stalls on security diligence) is the single highest-intent event but is low-observability — visible only to the vendor (panicked, P1's inbound, timing is a coin-flip), the FI's vendor-risk team, the auditor, and the GRC platform. Three of the four are partners. Therefore partner channels are not merely faster for the bridge; for catching the trigger in time they are structurally necessary — owned content captures the buyer only if the buyer happens to find it at the right week. This is an independent, capacity-grounded reason borrowed pipeline leads the early motion (P9), and it makes the R08 partner-exchange decision load-bearing for the whole strategy.
  • US/Canada timing (R02 D6; P7's carried dissent): US-first for volume; Canada/OSFI as a differentiation thread from day 1 (the dual-regime content and positioning run bilingually-aware from the start, since cross-border is the wedge), with a second origination wave (~12–18 mo) on the Streamlined-Framework cohort. P7's dissent is kept live and recorded: the Canadian tailwind is freshest now (Streamlined Framework launched ~Jun 2026), so a long Canada deferral risks forfeiting first-mover — gated on T6 (Streamlined cohort velocity) and revisited in R08 (CCUA/CBA channels).

Risk weight: the per-segment channel map is Medium (R04 E, accepted); the sequence is bound to R02 D6 (accepted). The bridge-trigger detection argument is a Moderator-grade inference grounded in R04 E/F — strong but not measured; bound to T1 (bridge demand) and R04 #4 (partner yield). Skeptic: accepted as sharpening; partner cooperation is thin at launch and thickens with references — the early funnel may not rely on it alone.

D23 — The expertise-led origination engine: the manufactured-referability flywheel, scoped to the wedge and instrumented

The owned engine is expertise-led origination that manufactures referability — not a referral motion (referrals decline, R04 A) and not a content-for-vanity motion (P12's condition). Its hub is the Bridge Engagement.

  • The flywheel: publish the dual-regime vendor-diligence/examiner-defensibility judgment (the wedge, R02/R04 C) → get found by the 95%-not-in-market bridge buyer and arm the existing network with something concrete to refer → land a Bridge Engagement → the engagement produces the dual artifact (R05 D14: SaaS deal-unblock story + examiner-legible reference) → the reference is the next buyer's peer-referral proof (R07 §B) and the next content case and the next partner's reason to route → repeat. The flywheel manufactures the referrals the market no longer gives freely.
  • Content/demand-gen is scoped to the narrow wedge, not broad (R04 C; P12's binding condition): WNW publishes to dominate the mindshare of dual-regime vendor diligence and examiner-defensibility for the software-to-bank trust chain — narrow enough that a boutique can plausibly own it — written to the buyer's frame (P1; T7), at a sustainable founder cadence (P3: consistency compounds; a launch blitz that dies does not).
  • Instrumented from day one with a kill-criterion (R04 #2; the Skeptic's condition): inbound-attributed pipeline and content-influenced win rate are measured from launch. The content engine is a metered experiment, not a faith-based budget line; if the niche-content-to-pipeline rate does not materialize within a defined window, the engine reweights to partner + referral. The firm loses a founder time-block, not the business.
  • What the engine is NOT: not cold outbound (3–6%, supplement only, R04 D); not a referral-dependency (declining asset, R04 A); not broad-market content (vanity outside a niche, R04 C / P12).
